Approval
This thesis has been prepared over six months at the Department of Measurement and
Instrumentation from DTU Space at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial
fulfilment for the degree Master Electrical Engineering, MSc Eng.
It is assumed that the reader has a basic knowledge in the areas of electronics and soft­
ware development.
ii
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This Master Thesis addresses the Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat simulation
platform, composed of the following subsystems: Communication Subsystem, On­Board
Computer Subsystem, Mechanical Structure, Ground segment, and a prototype subsys­
tem. First, this work presents a study of CubeSat standards in industry that are later
applied to the design process of each subsystem. This report concludes with the inte­
gration of all the components into a functional CubeSat capable to communicate with the
ground segment.
The project follows a two­fold approach: developing an educational tool that gets students
closer to CubeSat technology in which they can learn using a real system; on the other
hand, providing a complete simulation platform for students and researchers to test new
technologies before launching at an affordable price.
This project will require the application of methodologies used in the aerospace industry
in order to minimize risks and achieve a successful development of the CubeSat.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform

Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
xi
==PAGE:14==
1
Introduction
Over the past few decades, satellites have grown in size, cost, and complexity. This trend
has greatly increased the capabilities of satellites, however, technological advancement
is still limited by the risky, high­cost nature of space missions. As the demand for more
advanced spacecraft increases, a need for cheap low­risk on­orbit simulation platforms
has risen. Smaller satellites have become increasingly popular in the aerospace industry
and have allowed non­governmental organizations to develop space technologies.
CubeSats, a type of small satellite weighing less than 1.33 kg, are a popular low­cost plat­
form for academic and some government missions. CubeSats can support a variety of
mission types, including biological research, communications, deep space observation,
or technology testing and characterization. These spacecrafts have high educational po­
tential.
Despite the increased accessibility to CubeSats, the overall cost and development time of
these spacecraft is still high. The CubeSat program has improved the situation, but these
spacecraft still require a high level of technology development. There are several third­
party solutions, including companies that manufacture flight­ready CubeSat subsystems,
but these products cost thousands of dollars, excluding small companies and educational
institutions from being competitive in today’s industry. These facts highlight the need for
accessible technologies in CubeSats development, in order to create low­cost science
missions in space.
1.1
Problem statement
The experience from the past missions at DTU, and other universities, with CubeSats
state that there is a lack of testing and integration methods [1] for CubeSats. The high
failure among student­driven CubeSat projects are often attributed to a lack of adherence
to best practices and standards. Frequently, the complexity of even a ”simple” space­
craft is underestimated, leading to significant delays during development. Consequently,
insufficient resources are allocated for environmental and functional testing.
The goal of this Master Thesis is to design and manufacture a 1 unit CubeSat Simulation
Platform that complies with the main standards of the CubeSat industry. From an educa­
tional point of view, the developed work should help students to understand the concepts
of Spacecraft technology and serve as a basis for new CubeSats developments.
1.2
Motivation
The high failure rate of university CubeSats missions, due mainly to the lack of testing
and integration methods [1], can be improved with a realistic educational CubeSat. There
are several reasons behind the need to develop a new Educational CubeSat:
• Create an open­source satellite that can serve as a reference for future development
An open­source CubeSat platform can serve as a valuable resource for the educa­
tional and scientific community. This can lead to the development of more advanced
and reliable CubeSat technologies, as well as the standardization of methodologies
and components. It also provides a starting point for institutions or individuals who
wish to develop their own CubeSats, reducing the time and effort required to start
from scratch.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
1
==PAGE:15==
• Create a professional tool that complies with the main CubeSat standards
By adhering to the main CubeSat standards, the developed tool will not only be suit­
able for educational purposes but also for professional use. This ensures that stu­
dents are learning with a tool that is relevant and applicable to the industry, preparing
them for potential future careers in the field. Furthermore, a tool that complies with
industry standards can be used for the testing and validation of external CubeSat
components, contributing to the development of more reliable CubeSat missions.
• Reduce costs of CubeSats developments
By creating a cost­effective CubeSat simulation platform, the financial barrier to de­
velopment is significantly reduced. This allows more institutions to provide hands­on
CubeSat education, potentially leading to an increase in the number of successful
CubeSat missions. Furthermore, the use of a simulation platform can reduce the
costs associated with testing and validation, as potential issues can be identified
and addressed before the actual manufacturing process.
• Have realistic educational material for space technology
The use of realistic educational material is crucial in providing students with a com­
prehensive understanding of space technology. Students would be able to gain
practical experience and a deeper understanding of the complexities and challenges
associated with CubeSat development. This hands­on experience gives practical
and theoretical knowledge and prepares students for real­world applications.
1.3
Objectives
The main objective of this Master Thesis is to design and implement a 1U CubeSat Sim­
ulation platform that adheres to industry standards. The platform is founded as an edu­
cational tool, providing a realistic approximation of a CubeSat with its main functions and
subsystems while it is easy to understand by university students. The specific objectives
of the project are:
• Design and implement an On­Board Computer subsystem based on DTUSAT 3
Consisting of designing a Printed Circuit Board (PCB) that would integrate the DTUSAT
OBC into the CubeSat platform proposed in this work. The integration of the OBC’s
peripherals with the Kernel Linux is a critical step, necessitating the creation of a
software implementation for the OBC.
• Design and implement the Communications Subsystem based on wireless commu­
nication
The COM subsystems need to be developed to facilitate communication between
the satellite and the ground segment. They need to be meticulously designed and
implemented to ensure robust and reliable communication between the satellite
and the ground segment. This involves selecting appropriate wireless communica­
tion technologies, ensuring compatibility, and optimizing for range and data transfer
rates.
• Design and implement a Ground Station that connects to the Cubesat and receives
the Telemetry and sends the Telecommands
The ground segment needs to be designed to receive information from the satellite,
enabling it to plot different sensor data and send telecommands to the satellite.
2
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:16==
Additionally, the ground station should be equipped to send telecommands to the
satellite, allowing for real­time control and adjustments. The interface should be
user friendly, ensuring that operators can easily plot sensor data, monitor satellite
status, and issue commands as needed.
• Design and implement the 1U Mechanical structure
It is essential to design and implement a Mechanical Structure in which the different
subsystems will be mounted. This structure needs to follow the CubeSat standard
and should be easy to manufacture.
• Integration of the different subsystems
The assembly of all the different subsystems into a complete CubeSat is necessary
to validate the functionality of the design. The subsystems require to communicate
with the other subsystems in a network, and the satellites must be able to commu­
nicate with the ground segment.
1.4
Methodology and Schedule
The Gantt Diagram for the project is shown in Appendix M and it is described below.
The first part of the schedule starts with a preliminary study of the technology developed
at DTUSAT and getting familiar with the experience from past missions. This step aims
to understand the challenges that students face when working on a CubeSat, making
the correct foundational pillars for the project being developed. Secondly, it is going to
be performed the development of the Communications (COM) subsystem, that later will
serve use with the communication of the system. After this is done, it is the phase of the
development of the Ground segment subsystem, in which we can test the COM subsystem
that was previously developed. Then, the next step is the design and development of
the On­Board Computer (OBC) subsystem, reusing the technology of DTUSAT. The last
section of the practical part is the integration of the different subsystems into a complete
CubeSat. Then the project is stopped for 3 weeks, due to another mandatory course at
DTU that I needed to complete. The final phase of this Master Thesis is dedicated to
the writing process, having enough time to write in detail all the concepts that have been
developed.
1.5
Resources
For this project, the following resources will be used are:
• OBC of DTUSAT 3
The On­Board Computer (OBC) of DTUSAT 3 will be used as the main processing
unit for the CubeSat simulation platform. This OBC has been proven in previous
DTUSAT missions and provides the necessary computational power and interfaces
for the simulation platform. The hardware is ready to use, with some know errors.
However the software is not implemented, making it necessary to configure the OS
to have it working.
• Solidworks
Solidworks is a powerful 3D CAD design software. It will be used in this project for
the design of the mechanical structure and assembly drawings of the CubeSat.
• Altium designer
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
3
==PAGE:17==
Altium Designer is an electronic design automation software that will be used for
the design of CubeSat’s electronic circuits and systems. It is used this software
because is a professional tool used in the industry.
• Technical equipment and resources
For the manufacturing and implementation of this Master Thesis project, it is neces­
sary oscilloscope, 3D printer, power supply, soldering station, etc. The laboratory
of DTUSAT at the Technical University of Denmark provides the equipment for this
project. It is worth mentioning that the company Faunaphotonics has graciously
agreed to supply the necessary components for this project. This development is
possible thanks to their implication in the project.

Structure of the report
The content of this work is organized as follows:
• Chapter 1 present the engineering challenge, as well as the motivations and objec­
tives for this project, followed by a specification of requirements that every subsys­
tem must fulfill.
• In section 2, it is introduced the topic and present the developments done in the
industry.
• In Section 3, it is discussed the relevant theory to the topic by introducing differ­
ent concepts, technologies, and guidelines that are needed in order to understand
the approach taken for the design and implementation of this CubeSat Simulation
Platform.
• The fourth section, Section 4, describes the process of designing and implementing
the developed CubeSat Simulation Platform.
• Chapter 5 present the result of this work with a high­level description of its function­
alities.
• Chapter 6 concludes the design and implementation of the CubeSat.
• Chapter 7 discusses the issues and opportunities that were found during the devel­
opment of the project and summarizes tasks for future work.
• The appendices contain PCB schematics and layouts of the different boards devel­
oped for the design and the source code of the different systems.

Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:18==
2
State of Art
2.1
Background study: CubeSats
The CubeSat specification was developed in 1999, by California Polytechnic State Univer­
sity and Stanford University, in order to take a new approach to satellites as an educational
tool to involve students in working on a real space project. These small satellites typically
operate in Low Earth Orbit (LEO) performing scientific research and testing new space
technologies. The main goal of CubeSat specifications was to standardize the CubeSat
physical structure so as to make it compatible with a common orbital deployer; the most
used is Poly­Picosatellite Orbital Deployer (P­POD), having launched most of the Cube­
Sats since 2007. This type of spacecraft is U­class, meaning that the satellite can be
composed of various units linked together. Each Unit (1U) has a physical dimension of a
cube with an edge of 10 cm and a mass of less than 1.33 kg.
This standardization make it possible to launch multiple CubeSats at once, there was no
need to specifically design a new launch craft, highly reducing the cost. This concept
opened an inexpensive opportunity for companies and universities to launch their own
satellites in a cost­effective way. They allowed us to incorporate new ideas and concepts
into missions with fewer restrictions. The reduced price of launching CubeSats allows
universities to perform more and more frequent launches.
Figure 2.2 shows a bar graph presenting the number of NanoSatellites launched by year
and type, confirming the increasing launches of this type of satellites. The increasing
number of CubeSats missions led to the creation of new companies with the purpose of
developing and selling their hardware for CubeSats, like ISIS, EnduroSat, or GOMspace.
These companies offer the whole satellite, or they can simply provide a subsystem, like
the EPS, COMMS, etc.
Figure 2.1: Nanosatellites launched until 2021 [2]
CubeSat projects, driven by students at universities, suffer from a high failure rate ex­
ceeding the 60% [1]. This can be attributed to a lack of adherence to best practices and
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
5
==PAGE:19==
standards. Frequently, the complexity of even a ”simple” spacecraft is underestimated,
leading to significant delays during development. Consequently, insufficient resources
are allocated for environmental and functional testing. This low success rate is usually
related to the lack of integration and testing of the subsystems of the CubeSats [3].
A CubeSat is composed of different subsystems, each of them carrying out a specific task
of the mission. The different subsystems are:
• On­Board Computer (OBC): The main subsystem of a CubeSat. It facilitates all core
flight functionality and implements all major decision­making and monitoring of all
subsystems. It is typically based on a central microprocessor or computing core.
• Communications Subsystem (COMMS): It is in charge of sending telemetry and
receiving telecommands. Sometimes is the most critical subsystem, if there is no
contact with the satellite, the mission can be considered lost. It is composed of
an antenna system, a transceiver, RF electronics, amplifiers, filtering, and signal
couplers, among many other elements.
• Attitude Determination and Control Subsystem (ADCS): It is dedicated to get the
orientation of the satellite and correct it. It can be composed of passive actuators,
like magnetic rods, or active actuators, like reaction wheels or magnetorquers.
• Electrical Power Subsystem (EPS): This subsystem provides, stores, distributes,
and controls CubeSat’s electrical power. It is composed of solar panels, batteries,
voltage regulators, switches, etc.
Additionally, a CubeSat counts with a Payload subsystem, which is the main purpose of
the mission. The data acquired from the experiment carried on the spacecraft will be sent
as telemetry by the COMMS. There are plenty of payloads like cameras, radiometers,
sensors...
2.2
Commercially available solutions
In this section, some examples of CubeSat and SmallSat platforms of the last decade are
presented. These are projects that were meant to be commercially­available solutions,
that have a similar approach as this project. This small study will provide us with inspiration
and will help us to identify the missing functionalities that could present opportunities for
the project.
2.2.1
ESAT
ESAT [4] is an educational CubeSat accounting for the major elements of a real mission,
created by Theia Space. It is a realistic simulation of a real satellite used for a wide variety
of education and scientific activities. Currently, this project is not active anymore.
It is composed of two magnetorquers, one inertial motor, Wi­Fi communication, two solar
panels, and sun sensors, among other elements. It is worth mentioning that is based on a
microcontroller using standardized Telemetry and Telecommand (TMTC) definition, using
CCSDS and XTCE. Figure 2.2 shows a picture of the ESAT CubeSat.
The communication between the CubeSat and the ground station is done via Wi­Fi tech­
nology using a TCP connection. ESAT is a complete solution that has a realistic model of
a CubeSat, however, its main parts are private­source and the project is not active. This
solution of Theia Space costs around 7000 €[4].

2.2.2
XK90 - CubeSat XinaBox Kit
XK90 [5] is an educational CubeSat kit developed by XinaBox. Its main intention is to
introduce space technologies to students with the STEM methodology. It is an interesting
approach to educational CubeSats, but it is far from a real satellite. Figure 2.3 shows a
picture of the assembled device, the CubeSat on the left, and the ground segment on the
right.
Figure 2.3: XK90 CubeSat by XinaBox [5]
This solution is based on xChips, an electronics development ecosystem that simpli­
fies electronic circuits by interconnecting elements and removing the need for soldering.
There are a large number of xChips available in XinaBox with a wide range of uses, like
sensors, batteries, transceptors, etc. The XK90 contains the most basic elements of a
CubeSat in xChips. Some functionalities of this solution are Lora communication, weather
sensor, IMU, GPS, Antenna, etc.
While it is an attractive approach of CubeSat by dividing each element into a different
xChip, it is far from the CubeSat specification [6] and from a real satellite. Its price is
497.27 €.

2.2.3
RASCube Educational Satellite Kit
The RASCube, developed by Robinson Aerospace Systems [7], is an innovative educa­
tional satellite kit designed to inspire students to explore careers in the space industry.
The RASCube is a satellite model that is designed to mimic the look, feel, and function
of a real CubeSat, but it is not intended for space deployment. The kit comes in a flat­
pack format, providing students with everything they need to assemble and use their own
satellite in a classroom setting. The RASCube wirelessly transmits live data, such as GPS
position, orientation, acceleration, magnetic fields, temperature, and pressure, which can
be displayed on a computer screen.
The kit includes five circuit boards: the Electrical Power System (EPS), On­Board Com­
puter (OBC), Sensor Module, Telemetry Module, and Prototyping Board. The Sensor
Module includes an IMU, barometer, magnetometer, GPS, and various power sensors.
The RASCube also includes six high­efficiency solar cells to charge its two lithium­ion
batteries, providing power to the satellite’s electronic systems.
The RASCube is designed with expandability and modularity in mind, allowing students
to develop their own electronic modules compatible with the RASCube. The satellite is
programmed through the Arduino IDE, making it a versatile tool for students interested in
space technology. The RASCube wirelessly sends data to a USB­style receiver, which
can be plugged into Windows or MacOS computers. This data is then displayed in live
graphs and graphics using Robinson Aerospace’s proprietary User Interface. Students
can also download the data to a Microsoft Excel spreadsheet for further analysis.
This device is under development and not possible to buy yet. It is priced at $2200, the
RASCube is primarily marketed as an educational tool for high school students. While
it adheres to the mechanical standards of a CubeSat, its software and PCB design do
not follow industry recommendations. This means that although it serves as an excellent
learning platform, it is not designed to function as a simulation CubeSat environment
for testing real subsystems. Its proprietary design limits its adaptability for use in actual
CubeSat missions.
Figure 2.4: RASCube educational satellite [7]

2.2.4
PyCubed
PyCubed [8] is an open­source, radiation­tested SmallSat framework that is entirely pro­
grammable in Python. This platform offers an innovative approach to SmallSat develop­
ment, leveraging the simplicity and versatility of Python to provide a user­friendly interface
for satellite programming.
The PyCubed platform is designed with a focus on accessibility and ease of use, making
it an ideal tool for educational purposes as well as for professional satellite development.
The platform includes both hardware and software components, providing a comprehen­
sive solution for SmallSat design and implementation. The hardware is designed to be
robust and radiation­tested, ensuring that it can withstand the harsh conditions of space.
On the other hand, the software and firmware are designed to be easy to use and flexible,
allowing for a wide range of applications.
One of the key features of PyCubed is its extensive resources and support. The platform
provides a wealth of resources for users, including a hands­on quick start guide for those
who are new to the platform. Additionally, PyCubed also has a dedicated forum where
users can discuss and share their experiences, providing a supportive community for
both new and experienced users. Despite its simplicity, PyCubed does not compromise
on functionality, making it a powerful tool for SmallSat missions.
The PyCubed device is offered as all­in­one solution, it works for a wide range of function­
alities such as On­Board Computer (OBC), Electrical Power Subsystem (EPS), Commu­
nications (COM), Command and Data handling, and also as payload interface. However,
it does not adhere to space industry standards, as it lacks of a stackable connector for
adding more subsystems. This device is designed for professional use, requiring a cer­
tain level of expertise in embedded software design and electronics design. Rather than
being an educational tool, it is more like an evaluation board that can be tailored for other
missions. Although it is not currently available for purchase, the estimated price is around
700€.
Figure 2.5: PyCubed SmallSat Framework PCB [8]

a platform would not only bridge the gap in the current market but also serve as an invalu­

experience with CubeSat design and operations.

3
Theory
3.1
CubeSat standard
The most accepted CubeSat specification was defined by California Polytechnical State
University [6] in 1999. The goal was to create a standardized mechanical structure of
CubeSats which may be stacked into a launcher tube, called Poly­Picosatellite Orbital
Deployer (P­POD). This deployer is the interface between the launch vehicle and Cube­
Sats which would help reduce operational costs. This specification defines the measures
that a CubeSat has to follow in order to be compatible with the P­POD. Figure 3.7 shows
the measures of the +X face referred in the specification.
Figure 3.1: 1U CubeSat Cal Poly specification [6]
The specification defined by California Polytechnical State University is focused on the
mechanical structure design, hence the PCB layout does not follow any design rule. How­
ever, the most accepted PCB layout in CubeSats is the one defined by Pumpkin Corpo­
ration [9], defined in the CubeSat Kit PCB Specification, which defines exact measures
of the PCB layout as same as the coordinates of buses and drill holes. Figure 3.2 shows
the Pumpkin CubeSat PCB layout specification.
3.2
Interconnection of the different subsystems
Modularity is a key principle that drives the design and development of CubeSats. It
maximizes the use of common parts and reduces the complexity of missions by dividing
them in smaller tasks. This approach is particularly beneficial in student­run projects,
where challenges can be efficiently split and isolated across different subsystems, leading
to accelerated system development. It leads to the concept of ”Test­once”, defined as the
ability to test an application at the application level without the knowledge of the end
system and then never having to test the application again regardless of the number and
types of deployments of that application into completed S/W systems.
However, modularity also gives rise to additional challenges, such as the physical inter­
connection of various systems and the establishment of suitable communication protocols
among different subsystems. Currently, there is a lack of standardized approaches for fa­
cilitating communication between different subsystems. NASA conducted a study about
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
11
==PAGE:25==
Figure 3.2: PCB layout proposed by Pumpkin [9]
modular hardware and software standardization on small satellites [10]. It concludes with
high­level guidelines which could reduce the cost of SmallSat missions by introducing
standards. It is highlighted some important points: prioritize the need to implement stan­
dard interfaces at a lower integration level, adopt common communication buses for inter­
system communication, and modular approaches to reduce time of development.
This modularity is achieved in Cubesats by interconnecting the different boards with a
board­to­board connector. This is often achieved by stacking the boards on top of each
other, following the PC104 standard, or by inserting them into a shared board that acts as
an integrated backplane/connector, as demonstrated by the University of Würzburg with
the UWE­3 bus [11]. This way it is being reduced the use of wires inside the satellite and
limiting the difficulties related to the cabling.
One of the most common standards for CubeSat electronics is the PC104 form factor. It
allows developers to stack the boards one on top of the other, facilitating the connection
between the different boards. The use of this standard constrains the satellite’s internal
design, but at the same time, the use of standoffs between the boards helps to increase
the structural performance of the satellite itself. This type of connector has been used on
the GOMSpace designs [12], and in a variety of missions as in the QB50 [13].
Another approach for interconnecting subsystems is the one used in the DTUSat­3 mis­
sion which involves the use of a 40­pin connector, in which each subsystem is connected
to a ”Carrier board”. This design philosophy effectively reduces the size of individual con­
nectors, resulting in a more compact overall system.
After evaluating various connector options for interconnecting subsystems, including the
40­pin connector of DTUSat­3, the UWE­3 connector, and the full PC104 connector used
by GOMSpace, it is evident that the PC104 with one header containing 52 pins is the
most suitable choice. The DTUSat 40­pin connector lacks sufficient spacing between the
carrier board and the subsystem, and it is challenging to solder and access the different
pins. The UWE­3 connector does not adhere to the PCB layout standards of Pumpkin in
figure 3.2. On the other hand, the full PC104 connector, shown in figure 3.5, although is
12
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:26==
Figure 3.3: DTUSat­3
Figure 3.4: UWE­3 Bus[14]
Figure 3.5: GOMSpace PC104[12]
Figure 3.6: Different connectors types for the interconnection of the subsystems
widely used by the manufacturers, it is rarely used that high amount of pins for a bus in a
CubeSat mission.
Opting for the PC104 with a single header (52 pins) offers several advantages. It is easily
accessible, simple to implement, occupies a relatively small area and it follows the Pump­
kin PCB layout. Although the pins may bend when connected and disconnected multiple
times, this minor inconvenience is outweighed by the overall benefits of this connector
choice.
3.3
Data-link communication between subsystems
A spacecraft is composed of multiple subsystems which are transferring information be­
tween each other. Data is transferred through the spacecraft using a variety of mecha­
nisms which can be divided into Point­to­point and Multidrop communication. In a point­to­
point topology, the sender communicates directly with a single receiver while a multidrop
topology allows for a sender to communicate with multiple receivers. The first strategy
is convenient for simple applications but difficult to adapt for larger applications with mul­
tiple subsystems. If a new application is added, the interfaces of the application and all
applications that the new application talks to may have to be modified. However, in a
multidrop topology, all applications have the same interface, and the addition or removal
of new applications has no effect on existing applications.
In a CubeSat mission in which modularity must be pursued, the utilization of multidrop
communication protocols is strongly recommended. A comparative analysis will be per­
formed to determine the most suitable data­link communication protocol that aligns with
the mission’s requirements. The protocols that are going to be compared are i2C and
CAN.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
13
==PAGE:27==
Figure 3.7: Left: Point to point application; Right: Multidrop application
CAN
Controller Area Network (CAN) is a communication protocol widely used in automotive
and industrial applications. It is based on a multi­master and multi­slave communication
network, which allows multiple devices to communicate efficiently. One of its key char­
acteristics is the high reliability, achieved through error correction mechanisms. It is a
message­based protocol, which is composed primarily of an identifier and the data, with
a maximum of 8 bytes. The message identifier ensures the timely and efficient transmis­
sion of critical data.
The CAN protocol is defined by the ISO standard 11898­1 [15]. The standard is specified
for lower two layers of the Open Systems Interconnection (OSI) standard, levels 1 and
2 for physical and data link layers. In the physical layer, it uses a differential signaling
scheme, known as differential voltage signaling, where data is represented by the voltage
difference between two wires: CAN_H and CAN_L. In the data link layer, it is defined the
structure of CAN frames, including the frame start and end, identifier, data length, and
other control fields. The primary feature of the CAN protocol is the fixed message length
of 8 bytes. Consequently, when dealing with larger messages, it becomes essential to
implement fragmentation and reassembly techniques to handle the data effectively.
In order to interface with a CAN­based network it is necessary two elements, a CAN
controller and a CAN transceiver. On the one hand, the CAN controller is responsible for
the data link layer functions, such as frame encoding and decoding, error detection, and
error handling. On the other hand, the CAN transceiver acts as an interface between the
CAN controller and the physical medium. It converts the digital signals from the controller
into appropriate electrical signals for transmission over the CAN bus and vice versa.
I2C
Inter­Integrated Circuit (I2C) is a communication protocol commonly used in embedded
systems for short­distance, intra­board communication. It is a multi­master and multi­
slave protocol characterized by its simplicity and low­speed data transfer. The protocol
supports data sizes up to 8 bits and allows for the connection of multiple devices with just
two wires: a data line (SDA) and a clock line (SCL).
The I2C protocol is defined by the I2C­bus specification and user manual [16]. It operates
at the physical and data link layers of the Open Systems Interconnection (OSI) model.
The physical layer of I2C uses a bi­directional open­drain or open­collector bus, which
includes pull­up resistors to restore the signal to a high state when no device is pulling it
low. The data link layer defines the structure of the I2C data units, which include a start
condition, a stop condition, an address frame, and one or more data frames. The address
frame carries the 7­bit address of the slave device and the data direction bit (read or write).
To interface with an I2C­based network, a device must have an I2C controller and a phys­
ical layer transceiver. The I2C controller handles the generation of start and stop con­
ditions, sends and receives data, and manages the acknowledgment bits. The physical
14

SDA and SCL lines, and for the generation of the clock signal.
Comparison
Given these considerations, the decision has been made to use the CAN protocol for
interconnecting the different subsystems. Despite its complexity and data size limitation,
the CAN protocol’s high reliability and efficient multi­device communication make it a more
suitable choice for this application.
3.4
Network layer
In the previous sections we have explored the first two layers of the Open Systems In­
terconnection (OSI) model: the Physical layer (Layer 1) and the Data Link layer (Layer
2). These layers are fundamental for establishing the physical connection between de­
vices and ensuring reliable data transfer across this connection. However, in situations
where various subsystems need to communicate with each other, it becomes essential
to take into account higher­level protocols, particularly the Network layer, also known as
OSI Layer 3.
The Network layer is responsible for packet forwarding, including routing through differ­
ent networks and across network segments. This layer is crucial for managing network
congestion and determining the optimal path for data transmission. As an example to
illustrate the importance of this network layer, let’s consider the process involved in trans­
mitting a telecommand from a ground station to a specific subsystem of a CubeSat. In a
broad sense, the packet dispatched by the ground station must contain information about
the specific subsystem to which it is being sent, the destination. To facilitate this, it is used
a routing mechanism which ensures that the packet is correctly and efficiently directed to
its intended subsystem.
In the context of CubeSat communication, two protocols stand out for their suitability and
widespread use in this layer: the CCSDS Space Packet and the CubeSat Space Proto­
col (CSP). Both of these protocols offer unique advantages and have been successfully
implemented in numerous CubeSat missions, [17] [18]. In the following sections, we will
delve into the specifics of each protocol, its implementation, and its role in the OSI model.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
15
==PAGE:29==
CubeSat Space Protocol
The CubeSat Space Protocol (CSP) is a small network protocol stack written in C, de­
signed specifically for CubeSats. It was originally created by students from Aalborg Uni­
versity and is now maintained by GOMSpace. CSP provides the equivalent of OSI layers
1, 2, 3, and optionally 4. It supports various physical layers for interconnecting different
subsystems, including CAN­bus, I2C, UART, and RS232.
At the Data Link Layer, CSP defines a frame format suitable for each communication
channel, ensuring reliable, error­free transmission of data frames. At the Network Layer,
CSP manages the routing of data packets between network nodes using routing tables in
each subsystem, this needs that the network topology needs to be decided in advance.
At the optional Transport Layer, CSP handles message segmentation and reassembly,
error detection and correction, flow control, and other transport layer functions. CSP is
written in C and ported to FreeRTOS and POSIX operating systems such as Linux. Its
simplicity makes it possible to implement in small systems, making it a suitable choice for
CubeSat missions.
CCSDS
The Consultative Committee for Space Data Systems (CCSDS) was founded in 1982 to
discuss and develop standards for space data and information systems. It is composed
of the world’s leading space agencies, including ESA, NASA, and JAXA. One of the most
important standards developed by this organization is the Space Packet Protocol, often
referred to as the CCSDS protocol. This standard specifies the communications protocol
to be used by space missions. Its main purpose is to transfer space application data
between a sending and a receiving entity, such as a ground station and a satellite, relying
on services provided by underlying layers.
The CCSDS protocol defines a specific packet format for telemetry data, as shown in
figure 3.8. This format ensures that data is correctly structured for transmission and re­
ception, facilitating communication between ground stations and satellites. The CCSDS
standard is widely adopted in the space industry and provides a robust and reliable frame­
work for space data communication.
In contrast to CSP, this protocol is defined for the Network Layer by describing a packet
format. It does not provide a ready­to­use implementation of the protocol. On the other
hand, it is extensively documented, providing comprehensive guidelines that make it
straightforward to implement across various ecosystems.
Figure 3.8: Space Packet Structural Components [19]

Comparison
The CubeSat Space Protocol (CSP) was initially proposed as the common framework for
all subsystems in this project. This choice was primarily influenced by several factors.
Firstly, CSP is specifically designed for CubeSats, making it an ideal choice. Secondly, it
is supported by the community under a Free Software license (MIT license) [20]. Thirdly,
it is developed by our college students of Aalborg University [21]. Lastly, CSP is designed
for embedded systems, aligning well with the technical requirements of our project.
However, the practical implementation of CSP presented some significant challenges.
When I attempted to compile the project for the ESP32 architecture and Arduino systems,
I encountered with some difficulties because it is built on top of the FreeRTOS framework.
CSP is designed with a monolithic structure making it highly difficult to be ported to other
frameworks like Arduino or ESP­IDF. This was a considerable setback, as the project’s
primary motivation is to create a system that is easily accessible to university students,
who are often familiar with the Arduino platform.
In contrast, CCSDS packets offer a higher degree of flexibility and adaptability. They
are highly abstract and can be implemented in a variety of ways, making them less re­
strictive than CSP. This means that they can be used with a wider range of architectures
and frameworks, not just those that are compatible with FreeRTOS. This flexibility is a
significant advantage, particularly for a project that aims to be open and accessible.
Given these considerations, it has been decided to use CCSDS packets instead of CSP
for this project. This decision is based on the need for a system that is both open and
simple, and that can be easily used by students familiar with platforms like Arduino. This
approach offers the best balance between adhering to CubeSat industry practices and
maintaining an open, abstract system. This decision arises a new challenge, that is the
need for an implementation of the CCSDS packets, which will be discussed in the design
and implementation section.
3.4.1
Fragmentation and reassembly
In section 3.3 we discussed the different Data­link communication layers that make pos­
sible the flow of data between the different subsystems. We concluded that the most
suitable communication protocol is CAN, however, it has a limitation in that it is defined
for a data payload of only 8 bytes [22] . This becomes a challenge when dealing with
larger messages, such as CCSDS packets, which exceed this limit. To overcome this
limitation, fragmentation and reassembly mechanisms need to be implemented. Various
solutions have been developed in the industry to address this issue.
One such solution is the ISO 15765­2, also known as ISO­TP [23]. This protocol allows for
the transport of messages that exceed the eight­byte maximum payload of CAN frames.
The protocol can handle messages of up to 4095 bytes. To accommodate larger data,
this protocol reserves the first byte of the CAN frame for addressing and signalization
purposes, thereby reducing the available space for actual data in each CAN frame to 7
bytes.
Another approach is the use of Controller Area Network Flexible Data­Rate (CAN­FD)
[24]. It improves the classic 8­byte data length to 64 bytes of data in each CAN message,
however, it requires the controller to be compatible with the FD CAN standard. While it is
well­accepted in the motor industry, not all microcontrollers, such as the ESP32, support
this standard, which limits its applicability.
The Service Data Object (SDO) protocol [25], a part of CANopen, also implements seg­
mentation and desegmentation of longer messages. SDO is used for setting and reading
18
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:32==

Disadvantages

on CAN

The CubeSat Space Protocol (CSP) fixed this limitation of CAN by creating a simple so­
lution called CAN Fragmentation Protocol (CFP). It uses the feature of CAN­2.0 of using
message identifiers of 29­bit by adding the data of the segmentation. This way is the
only proposed solution that keeps the original 8­byte data payload in each message with­
out adding additional overhead. It allows the fragmentation and reassembly of larger
messages, making it possible to transmit larger data packets over CAN. This makes it a
suitable choice for applications that require the transmission of larger data packets over
CAN, such as CubeSat missions. Table 3.3 shows a comparison between the different
methods.

3.5
On-Board Computer
The On­Board Computer (OBC) used in the project is inherited from the ongoing devel­
opment of the DTUSat­3 mission. The OBC design was created by Blanca Alejo as part
of her master thesis titled ”Design, Manufacturing, and Testing of the OBC for DTUSat­3”
[26].
Figure 3.9: PCB of the OBC for DTUSat­3
The OBC design is based on the PocketBeagle as its foundation by integrating the Octavo
System­in­Package (SiP). In addition to the original PocketBeagle design, the OBC incor­
porates a CAN transceiver, a stack connector, and a step­up converter. The Octavo SiP,
known as the OSD335x­SM SiP, combines a robust ARM Cortex­A8 processor, mem­
ory, power management capabilities, and various supporting elements within a compact
form factor. This SiP also includes native support for peripherals like Ethernet, USB, and
GPIO, making it highly adaptable for a wide range of applications. To facilitate software
development for students, the OBC employs the Linux operating system.
3.5.1
Linux boot process
This section provides an overview of the Linux image boot process involved in the Octavo
OSD335x, it is extensively described on application notes supplied by the manufacturer,
Octavo Systems [27] [28].
The Universal Boot Loader (U­Boot), a common method for booting Linux, carries out
all the necessary steps to load and boot the Linux kernel. However, the U­Boot envi­
ronment requires more memory than the AM335x’s internal memory can provide. As a
result, U­Boot is divided into a first­stage and second­stage bootloader. This division is
automatically done during the U­Boot building process, but the segments are loaded into
different parts of the boot image. Moreover, the AM335x processor within the OSD335x
employs a four­stage boot process to load and run the operating system. The four boot
stages for a standard Linux boot are detailed below and illustrated in Figure 3.10.
• Stage 1: ROM Bootloader
The first stage of the boot process is managed by the Read­Only Memory (ROM)
within the AM335x. This stage involves the ROM bootloader initializing the boot pe­
ripherals, such as the memory section, the watchdog timer, and the system clocks.
It then loads the next stage bootloader into the AM335x’s internal RAM and initiates
its execution.
20
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:34==
Figure 3.10: Linux Boot stages on OSD335x [27]
• Stage 2: Secondary Program Loader (SPL)
The second stage, corresponding to the first stage of the U­Boot bootloader, op­
erates entirely within the AM335x processor’s internal memory. At this point, only
the boot peripherals have been initialized. The SPL’s primary role is to initialize the
DDR3 memory within the OSD335x, load the full version of U­Boot into the DDR
memory, and start executing that code.
• Stage 3: U­Boot Bootloader
The third stage, equivalent to the second stage of the U­Boot bootloader, is respon­
sible for loading and starting the Linux kernel. To do this, U­Boot searches for a
uImage file in non­volatile memory, such as a micro­SD card. The uImage file con­
tains both the Linux kernel and a header that describes the kernel.
• Stage 4: Linux Kernel
The final stage involves starting the Linux kernel, which boots and configures the
Linux operating system. The kernel also loads and configures all necessary device
drivers to ensure the system operates correctly.
EEPROM during boot and board ID
Inside the OSD335x System­in­Package there is an EEPROM memory that is connected
through the I2C0 bus to the CPU. This memory has the identifier that allows U­Boot to
recognize the specific printed circuit board it operates on, enabling the appropriate con­
figuration and loading of software drivers. This enhances U­Boot’s flexibility, permitting a
single U­Boot image to serve multiple development platforms.
However, if U­Boot fails to locate the board ID in the EEPROM because is blank, unpro­
grammed, or absent, the U­Boot will not proceed to boot the Linux kernel. This issue is
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
21
==PAGE:35==
particularly problematic as this verification takes place before the initialization of the serial
console, meaning no boot messages will appear on the UART0 serial console.
Linux device tree
The Linux device tree is a data structure, organized in a hierarchical manner, that de­
scribed the hardware configuration of the system to the Linux operating system. During
the boot process, the Linux kernel uses the device tree’s information to identify hardware
components, load the appropriate drivers, and manage the hardware devices present in
the system. It is used the EEPROM ID to identify the correct drivers that are being loaded
with the device tree.
A Linux device tree begins with a root node and includes one level of child nodes along with
one or more levels of subsequent nodes, often referred to as ’children’ nodes. Each child
node corresponds to a hardware component of the processor, in this case, the AM335x
processor. Each child or children node can have only one parent, with the root node being
the only exception as it has no parent. In the context of the PocketBeagle board, each
child node represents a component of the AM335x processor, such as the CPU or the
I2C peripheral, among others. Each child node, on the other hand, represents a sub­
component of a child node or a device that is connected to the child node. For instance,
on the DTUSAT OBC board, the CAN Controller inside the AM335x processor needs to
be configured as a children node with the correct arguments, by referencing the pins of
the transceiver.
In the device tree file, it is necessary to configure the multiplex of the different pins. The
necessary information is given in the technical reference manual of the AM335x processor
[29], which has all the references of the registers that need to be configured. This proces­
sor has a pin multiplex, meaning that one pin can be configured for different functionalities.
For example, the signal spi1_d0 used for SPI communication can be configured in 3 dif­
ferent physical pins of the AM335x processor. This functionality lets us create complex
layouts with multiple communication interfaces.
It is possible to add modifications to the original device tree, with the concept of Overlays.
In the U­Boot, we can define overlays that are changes in the device tree that are added
after the boot­up. In an embedded Linux system we would have the base overlay, that
describes all the necessary components of the CPU to work, and then overlays that im­
plement additional functionalities. These overlays can be easily added and removed from
the u­Boot with the file /boot/uEnv.txt, which is a bootscript that adds the overlays to the
system.
3.6
Communication subsystem
The Communications Subsystem (COMMS) of a satellite is in charge of sending telemetry
and receiving telecommands. It is one of the most critical subsystems, if there is no contact
with the satellite, the mission can be considered lost. It is composed of an antenna system,
a transceiver, RF electronics, amplifiers, filtering, and signal couplers, among many other
elements.
3.6.1
Transceiver
A transceiver is the main part of the COMMS that is in charge of the radio communications.
It is the radio interface between the satellite and the ground station. The transceiver used
in this design is going to be the SX1272 [30], because it is inherited from the COMMS
of DTUSAT­3. This transceiver offers ultra­long­range communication with a low energy
consumption. It offers FSK, GFSK, MSK, GMSK, LoRa, and OOK modulations.
22
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:36==
The SX1272 transceiver is known for its exceptional sensitivity, which plays a crucial role
in its ability to receive and decode weak signals. Sensitivity refers to the minimum power
level at which the transceiver can detect and demodulate a signal with an acceptable
level of accuracy and reliability. In the case of the SX1272, its high sensitivity enables it to
detect even faint signals in challenging environments, such as long­range or low­power
applications. This sensitivity is achieved through filters, high performance Low Noise
Amplifier (LNA) and signal processing techniques, allowing the transceiver to amplify and
extract valuable information from weak incoming signals. Table 3.4 shows the different
sensitivities of each modulation.
Modulation
Bandwidth [KHz]
Data rate [bps]
Sensitivity [dBm]
FSK
5
1200
­119
OOK
5
1200
­123
LoRa
125
293
­137
Table 3.4: Maximum sensitivity of the SX1272
This new generation of transceivers introduced in 2019 adds the LoRa spread spectrum
modem which shows high improvements in comparison to the existing modulations based
on FSK and OOK. The LoRa modulation scheme improves the sensitivity by 8 dB better
than the equivalent data rate FSK with a low­cost, low­tolerance crystal reference. How­
ever, LoRa is developed and maintained by Semtech Corporation as a proprietary tech­
nology, restricting the ability of other companies or individuals to modify or fully understand
the inner workings of the technology.
For this work, it is chosen the SX1272 transceptor with the LoRa modulation because of
the low­power consumption and the high sensitivity of the LoRa modulation. The propri­
etary nature of the technology is a thread to have into account, but it is compensated by
its excellent performance in other CubeSat missions [31].
3.7
Ground Station Segment
Every mission needs at least a Ground Station in order to communicate with the satellite. A
real Ground Station is composed of antennas, a transceiver, power amplifiers, and filters.
The transceivers used on ground are similar to the ones that are on board of the satellite
but without a power and size limitations. It is common to have high power amplifiers on
ground stations, and populated with low noise amplifiers.
On this section we will going to focus on the ground station software. In section 3.4 it
is explained the standard CCSDS, that are the data structures exchanged between the
satellite and the ground segment. It is necessary to define the possible telemetry that the
satellite is able to send, and the telecommands that are used. In this sense a standard is
highlighted, the XML Telemetric and Command Exchange (XTCE).
3.7.1
XML Telemetric and Command Exchange
XML Telemetric and Command Exchange (XTCE) [32] is an XML based data model cre­
ated to share telemetry and telecommand definitions between different ground stations,
or even in different missions and organizations. A space mission has a certain number
of lifecycle phases supported by a variety of systems and organizations; telemetry and
command definitions must be exchanged among all of these phases, systems, and orga­
nizations.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
23
==PAGE:37==
A typical example of this process is between the spacecraft manufacturer and the spacecraft­
operating agency. The different organizations define the telemetry and command data in
a format much different than the other. This creates the need for a common telemetry def­
inition, increasing customization and the probability of error. Using a common exchange
format streamlines the process of transferring definitions from the satellite integrator to
the operations team and between ground systems supporting the same satellite. This re­
duces the need to develop mission­specific database import/export tools and enables the
re­utilization of command and telemetry database tools. XTCE has also been adopted as
a recommendation by the CCSDS.
3.7.2
Ground Station Software
It is common for space missions to have a ground station dedicated software, a dashboard
where you can schedule future telecommands, decode telemetry, process data, etc. The
software of the Ground Station for this project will be based on the environment Ball
Aerospace COSMOS and will count with different user interfaces and a basic telemetry
database.
COSMOS is a complete environment developed by Ball Aerospace that covers the full
lifecycle of the mission, as the same interface can be used from test to operation. This
open­source software written in Ruby provides a control system to interact with a variety
of embedded systems. It is compatible with major standards like CCSDS and XTCE.
Figure 3.11: Ball Aerospace Cosmos logo [33]
The connection between the target and the software is defined by an interface, which can
be defined by different protocols, such as TCP/IP, UART, GPIB, Firewire, etc. COSMOS
software aims to be modular by creating customizable portions of code that can be reused
for various phases of the mission. This interface describes how the target and the software
communicate with each other.
The connection between the CubeSat and the Ground Station will be via Wi­Fi, through
a TCP socket that connects to the Software COSMOS. A Ground Station Software will
be developed with this software and it will be able to decode received telemetry, send
telecommands, create graphs, and store previous telemetry.
24
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:38==
4
System Design and Implementation
4.1
System Block Diagram
In the theory section, we discussed different important areas of a CubeSat mission. In
this section, we are going to collect the decisions made rewarding to the integration of the
different subsystems within a satellite. We are going to explain how all these decisions
operate together to make a working CubeSat.
In section 3.2 it was discussed about different board­to­board connectors. After evaluating
various connector options, the PC104 with a single header (52 pins) was found to be the
most suitable due to its accessibility, simplicity, and compatibility with the Pumpkin PCB
layout.
In section 3.3 and 3.4.1 it was described the multidrop data transfer that is going to be used
in our design, based on a common bus. After comparing the CAN and I2C protocols for
the communication between the different subsystems, the CAN protocol was chosen for
its high reliability and efficient multi­device communication, despite its complexity and data
size limitation. However, it is necessary to implement the CAN Fragmentation Protocol
(CFP) for the fragmentation and reassembly of the packets.
In section 3.4 it was discussed the Network layer of the OSI model in the context of Cube­
Sat communication. After comparing the CubeSat Space Protocol (CSP) and the CCSDS
Space Packet, the latter was chosen for its flexibility, adaptability, and compatibility with a
wider range of architectures and frameworks, despite the need for custom development
for each mission.
And lastly, the last section relevant to this topic is the 3.6.1 in which it was discussed the
transceiver and the modulation used in the communication between the satellite and the
ground segment. It concludes that, despite the proprietary nature of the LoRa technology,
the SX1272 transceiver has been chosen for its excellent performance in other CubeSat
missions.
All the pieces of the puzzle have been collected in figure 4.1. It shows the block diagram
of the proposed system. As an example, it is depicted 3 subsystems in green color: Com­
munication, On­Board, and Power subsystems. These are connected to each other by the
PC105 connector represented in grey color. It is illustrated that the communication proto­
col between the subsystems is CAN Fragmentation Protocol (CFP) and CAN2, where is
connected using the CANH and the CANL signal lines. Lastly, it is shown that the com­
munication with the ground segment is done with WiFi and LoRa using the CCSDS Space
Packet protocol. The WiFi communication is going to be used for debugging purposes
and for short­distance communication. The LoRa is used for long­range transmissions.
Each subsystem is understood as an independent system with its own logic. All the sub­
systems create a system in which the data is flowing between the network. The design
principles of each subsystem are:
• Modularity: Each subsystem should be designed as a separate, self­contained
module. This allows for easier testing, troubleshooting, and replacement of individ­
ual subsystems. It also allows for greater flexibility in the design and configuration
of the spacecraft.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
25
==PAGE:39==
Figure 4.1: System block diagram
• Reliability: Space is a harsh environment, and the subsystems must be designed
to operate reliably under these conditions. This can involve the use of radiation­
hardened components, redundant systems, and error detection and correction tech­
niques.
• Efficiency: The resources available on a spacecraft are limited, including power,
weight, and volume. Therefore, each subsystem should be designed to perform its
function as efficiently as possible, using the minimum necessary resources.
• Interoperability: The different subsystems must be able to communicate and work
together effectively. This requires the use of standard interfaces and protocols, and
careful coordination in the design of the subsystems.
• Scalability: The design of the subsystems should be scalable, allowing for the
spacecraft to be upgraded or expanded in the future. This could involve the use
of modular components or the provision of extra capacity in the initial design.
• Safety: The subsystems should be designed to operate safely, both for the space­
craft itself and for the surrounding environment. This can involve the use of fail­safe
mechanisms, safety margins in the design parameters, and compliance with rele­
vant safety standards and regulations.
• Compliance: The subsystems must be designed to comply with all relevant stan­
dards and regulations. This can include technical standards, frequency allocation
regulations, and space debris mitigation guidelines.
• Cost­effectiveness: The design of the subsystems should aim to achieve the mis­
sion objectives at the lowest possible cost. This can involve the use of off­the­shelf
components, simple and robust design solutions, and efficient manufacturing and
testing processes.
4.2
PC104 Bus definition
As mentioned in section 3.1, the PCB layout proposed by Pumpkin includes the placement
of two PC104 headers. However, for the creation of this simulation platform of a 1U
CubeSat such number of pins is not needed, it is enough with one header of 52 pins. This
decision makes the PCB much cheaper and gives more space for other components in
the PCB. The power of every subsystem is distributed by the EPS in the PC104 header.
The proposed layout for the PC104 interface with 52 pins counts with 1 CAN Bus, 1 i2C
26
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:40==
Figure 4.2: PC104 bus definition
bus, 1 UART interface, 5V and 3V general supply pins, individual 5V and 3V3 for each
subsystem and 12 pins as To­Be­Defined. The distribution of the pins can be seen in
figure 4.2. It is specified individual power pins for each subsystem in order to monitor
the power consumption, and the possibility to turn On and Off each subsystem. It is
distributed the power pins of 3V3, 5V, and GND, in order to have easy access from the
different subsystems in case it is necessary.
4.3
Mechanical structure
The design of a mechanical structure for a Cubesat is a critical aspect of the project.
The structure serves as a protective shell for the electronics and provides support for the
attachment of essential components such as antennas and solar panels in the sides of
the CubeSat. The mechanical part of a CubeSat was discussed in section 3.1, in which it
was stated that the design is going to follow the standard of California Polytechnical State
University of CubeSats [6], and the PCB layout of Pumpkin CubeSat [9]. This section
shows the design process used for the mechanical structure, concluding with a detailed
presentation of the result.
The design of the mechanical structure is subject to several constraints and desired fea­
tures. Firstly, the structure must be 3D printable. This ensures that the design is eas­
ily replicable and manufacturable, with tolerances that are suitable for the scope of this
project. Secondly, the structure should maximize the space available for payloads and
subsystems, ideally providing 80% of usable space within the CubeSat.
Furthermore, the design must allow for the attachment of PCBs to all sides of the cube,
providing students with the flexibility to attach solar panels or antennas as needed. The
structure must also be robust enough to withstand vibrations. To achieve this, subsystems
are attached to the CubeSat using threaded rods, which add strength to the structure and
do not restrict the height of the subsystems.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
27
==PAGE:41==
The primary components used in the structure are M3 screws, M3 thread inserts, and
M3 threaded rods, chosen for their affordability and easy availability. The thread inserts
make more reliable threads than those that are directly 3D printed. The inserts are more
durable and can withstand higher torques and loads on the screws. In order to use this
part it is necessary to design a hole in the 3D­printed part. With a soldering iron, heat
the threaded insert while pressing it into the hole in the 3D­printed part. The heat melts
the plastic slightly, allowing the insert to be pushed into place. Once cooled, the plastic
solidifies around the insert, securing it in place. The threaded inserts are going to be used
for the union of the threaded rods to the chassis, and the anchors to the chassis with the
M3 screws.
Figure 4.3: M3 screw, threaded insert, and threaded rod
One challenge encountered during the design process was the close proximity of the
Pumpkin PCB layout to the edges. As illustrated in figure 4.4, the CubeSat standard
and the adopted PCB layout show some limitations. With all the parts centered, it gives
a separation of 2mm between the edge of the PCB and the end of the CubeSat limit,
restricting the width of the walls. While this might not be a significant issue in a metal
structure, it could potentially lead to mechanical failure in a 3D­printed plastic structure.
In order to fix this, the anchors of the CubeSat will have a shape of an L, with side panels
affixed to them. This design choice not only accommodates the close edge proximity but
also enhances the CubeSat’s structural integrity.
Figure 4.4: Green: PCB dimensions of Pumpkin PCB [9]. Blue: Maximum allowed face
size of a CubeSat [6]
The design process was carried out using SolidWorks, utilizing the assembly tool and
iterative prototyping. The 3D printable CubeSat structure designed for this Master Thesis
can be found in a Github repository for free download 1. A render of the structure can be
seen in figure 4.5, illustrated without side panels and with 3 green PCBs as an example.
1https://github.com/acien101/CubeSat­Printable­Mechanical­Structure
28
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:42==
Figure 4.5: Mechanical Structure render
The structure consists of three types of 3D­printed parts, the top chassis, the bottom
chassis, and the anchors. An exploded view of the CubeSat is shown in figure 4.6. The
bottom chassis, blue part, and the top chassis, yellow part, are used to fix the 4 threaded
rods that will support the different subsystems of the satellite, the anchors, and the top
and bottom side planes. The anchors, red parts, are used to support the side panels
and the top/bottom chassis. In the exploded view is shown the side panels with glass
material. The resulting structure is not only robust and versatile but also easily replicable
and affordable, making it an ideal solution for Cubesat projects.

4.4
CAN Fragmentation Protocol implementation
The framework CubeSat Space Protocol (CSP) uses the CAN communication protocol
to communicate the different subsystems, among other protocols. In section 3.4.1 we
discussed about different implementations of fragmentation and reassembly protocols that
fix the limitation of sending 8 bytes of payload in CAN messages. CSP fixed this issue
by creating a protocol called CAN Fragmentation Protocol (CFP), a simple fragmentation
and reassembly protocol.
The CFP is implemented in the code of CSP and barely documented with some comments
and some functions [34]. The original C code is based on a larger project that relies
on the FreeRTOS operating system, which complicates its reuse in different contexts or
with other operating systems. To address this, is necessary to implement the protocol
from scratch guided by the existing documentation. This approach allows us to create a
modular library that can be easily integrated into a variety of projects. In this section, we
will detail our implementation of the CFP for the Arduino platform, with an emphasis on
simplicity and accessibility for student use.
The CFP uses the features of CAN to add a layer of fragmentation and reassembly without
adding overhead, making it compatible with all the current systems based on CAN. The
second version of CAN, CAN­2.0B, has a unique 29­bit identifier in every message which
also represents the message priority. The information of fragmentation is embedded on
the id as shown in the figure 4.7.
SOURCE
DESTINATION TYPE
REMAIN
ID
5 bits
5 bits
1 bit
8 bits
10 bits
CAN FRAGMENTATION 
       PROTOCOL      
          29 bits
Figure 4.7: CAN Fragmentation Protocol (CFP) identifier description
The source and destination fields must match the source and destination addresses. With
a maximum of 16 different addresses, each subsystem will have a unique identifier. This
field is used to read messages that are directed to the correct destination, dropping the
remaining packets. The type field is used to distinguish the first and subsequent frames
in a fragmented CSP packet. Type is BEGIN (0) for the first fragment and MORE (1)
for all other fragments. The remain field indicates the number of remaining fragments,
and must be decremented by one for each fragment sent. The identifier field serves the
same purpose as in the Internet Protocol and should be an auto­incrementing integer to
uniquely separate sessions.
The ESP32 has a built­in CAN controller, that is referred to as Two­Wire Automotive In­
terface (TWAI) [35]. The manufacturer, Espressif, has created a driver for the controller
with a detailed description of its usage. The proposed implementation of CFP is based
on the TWAI driver of ESP32 to send and receive the CAN messages.
The library created for this Master Thesis is called ESP32­CFP and can be found on
Github [36], and listed on the Appendix H. It is written in C++ with the Arduino framework,
using the directory structure of Arduino. It has a directory with examples that explains the
main functionalities of the library. The main functions of this master thesis implementation
are sendCFP and receiveCFP. When you need to transmit data, you employ the sendCFP
function. This function takes the required arguments, constructs the CFP identifier, and
then sends the contents of the provided buffer as a sequence of fragmented messages.
On the flip side, when you are in the receiving mode, you utilize the receiveCFP function.
30
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:44==
This function continuously listens for available CAN packets. Upon detecting a packet,
it receives the complete CFP frame and returns the data, which has been reassembled
from the fragmented messages. This process ensures seamless transmission and re­
ception of larger data packets over the CAN network, all while maintaining a modular and
straightforward approach.
4.5
Communications Subsystem
During the section 3.6 it is discussed the requirements of the Communications (COM)
subsystem. We stated that this subsystem is going to be based on the ESP32­S3 micro­
controller and the SX1272 transceiver.
The SX1272 is going to be used with the RFM95, which is a PCB with castellated pins
that can be soldered directly to the PCB. The benefit of this is the easy implementation of
the system and the low cost.
4.5.1
COMMS Components
SX1272 and RFM95
The transceiver SX1272 is encapsulated on a QFN package with 28 pins. The interfaces
are 6 signal ports to access the registers, SPI bus, different radio ports, and power pins.
The manufacturer Semtech offer on the datasheet a recommended schematic of the com­
ponent [30]. As this transceiver is half­duplex it is necessary an RF switch that split the
transmission line when is transmitting than when is receiving. The radio port of the com­
ponent is not matched to 50 Ohms, as the transmission line, so it is necessary to add
some pi filters that adapt the impedance. Each port of transmission and reception has a
different pi filter to adapt the impedance. The recommended schematic can be seen in
figure 4.8. The design, manufacture, and budget of the design proposed in the SX1272
chip are increased by the number of different components that are involved. For this rea­
son, HopeRF introduced the RFM95 which is the implementation of this schematic on a
modular circuit making it simple to implement in a design.
The SX1272 transceiver is encapsulated in a 28­pin QFN package. It offers a variety of in­
terfaces including six signal ports to access the registers, an SPI bus, multiple radio ports,
and power pins. Semtech, the manufacturer, provides a recommended schematic for the
component in its datasheet [30]. This transceiver operates on a half­duplex structure,
forcing the use of an RF switch to alternate the transmission line between transmitting
and receiving modes. Furthermore, the radio ports of the SX1272 are not matched to
the standard 50­ohm transmission line impedance. This requires the addition of pi filters
to adapt the impedance, with each transmission and reception port requiring a distinct
pi filter for this purpose. The recommended schematic, which includes these additional
components, is depicted in figure 4.8.
However, the design, manufacturing, and budget considerations for the SX1272 are sig­
nificantly impacted by the complexity and number of additional components required for
its implementation. This complexity not only increases the cost but also the potential for
errors during the design and manufacturing process. In response to these challenges,
HopeRF introduced the RFM95 [37], a modular circuit that effectively implements the
SX1272 schematic in a small package. The RFM95 simplifies the design process by in­
tegrating the necessary components and functions into a single module. This simplified
design reduces the number of separate components required by reducing costs and po­
tential points of failure. The design of the communications subsystem is based on the
RFM95.
For interfacing the RFM95 transceiver of the COM subsystem it is used the Arduino LoRa
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
31
==PAGE:45==
Figure 4.8: SX1272 recommended schematic from Semtech [30]
Figure 4.9: RFM95 Lora Module [37]
library [38]. This open­source library is an easy­to­use tool and enables the implementa­
tion of the main features of the SX1272 transceiver. The library uses the SPI communica­
tion interface and the programming I/Os of the chip. It is designed to facilitate the sending
and receiving of data using LoRa radios, making it an excellent choice for the scope of
this project.
ESP32-S3
The ESP32­S3 is a highly integrated, high­performance Wi­Fi and Bluetooth System on
Chip (SoC) by Espressif Systems [39]. It features dual Xtensa 32­bit LX7 cores running
up to 240 MHz, with 512 KB of SRAM and 384 KB of ROM. It also includes a variety of
peripherals such as SPI, I2C, UART, I2S, PWM, and more. The ESP32­S3 is designed
for a wide range of applications, especially for low­power Internet of Things applications.
4.5.2
COMMS Block Diagram
The design philosophy behind the Communications (COM) subsystem is to create a sim­
ple and modular subsystem. The main goal of this subsystem is to respond to telecom­
mands and send telemetry with the transceiver. This modular philosophy consists in divide
32
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:46==
Figure 4.10: ESP32­S3­WROOM­2 Module by Espressif Systems [39]
complex problems into smaller ones, making them easy to implement and easy to test.
The only function of this COMM is to transmit all the received CAN packets to RF, and
transmit to the CAN bus all the messages it receives from RF. It could be understood as
a relay system, with a simple logic.
In this multidrop design, there is a common bus in which all subsystems are connected.
The communication of the COM with other systems, especially the OBC, is done by CAN
placed on the PC104 common bus, denoted with color grey at figure 4.11. The main part of
this design is the ESP32S3, the microcontroller connected to the CAN transceiver, and the
RFM95 LoRa transceiver. Both communications are with SPI. It is crucial to remember
the connection to the ESP32’s programming interface, which is essential for uploading
code to the system.
Figure 4.11: Block diagram of the COMMS
With this design we isolate the functionality of the COM subsystem, ensuring effectiveness
without the complications of other system interactions. Using modules like the ESP32 and
the RFM95 we guarantee a simple design with powerful features following the approach
of the project.
4.5.3
COM Software
The software of the Communications (COM) subsystem was created for this master thesis.
It is written in C++ using the Arduino framework, for the previously described ESP32S3
microcontroller. In the next paragraphs, we will be referring to the source code of the
COM subsystem, which is listed in the Appendix H.1. It is designed with two operational
modes, supporting both LoRa and WiFi communication, although not simultaneously. The

primary functionality of the code is twofold. Firstly, it listens for incoming telecommands
over the radio, LoRa, or WiFi. Upon receiving data, it transmits this information over the
common CAN bus using the CAN Fragmentation Protocol (CFP), described in section
4.4. Secondly, it monitors the CAN bus for incoming telemetry. When data is available,
it is sent over either LoRa or WiFi, depending on the mode currently in use. Figure 4.12
shows a simplified flow model of the software at the COM subsystem.
Figure 4.12: Simplified flow model diagram of the software of COM
Several libraries are required for the operation of the software. The WiFi library enables
the establishment of WiFi connections and the creation of a TCP server for data transmis­
sion. The LoRa library facilitates communication over the LoRa network. The CFP library
is used to handle the segmentation and reassembly of CAN messages, allowing for the
transmission of larger data packets over the CAN network. Lastly, the SPI library is used
for communication with the CAN transceiver.
For the correct networking of the subsystem among the other subsystems connected to
the CAN bus, it is necessary to define the identifier of the device and the identifier of the
possible received and transmitted messages. This information is part of the CFP header
fields of each message. The COM subsystem will have the identifier 1, and the possible
messages are listed in table 4.1 and 4.2
34
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:48==
Incoming CFP commands include PING, which prompts a PONG response and com­
mands to retransmit data over WiFi or LoRa. Outgoing CFP commands include PONG,
sent in response to a PING command, and telecommand messages received from the
Communications subsystem and sent to the On­Board Computer (OBC). The PING and
PONG messages serve a diagnostic function, enabling the verification of the subsystem’s
responsiveness to incoming communications.
In conclusion, the source code for the Communications subsystem is a robust and flexible
solution for managing the communication needs of a CubeSat. It provides a simple solu­
tion for transmitting and receiving data over both WiFi and LoRa networks. The use of the
library CFP, created for this Master thesis, ensures efficient handling of CAN messages,
and the system’s adherence to a modular design principle. This proposed software serves
as an example representation of a COM subsystem that adheres to industry standards.
