4.6
Prototype subsystem
During the development of this master thesis, it was presented the challenge to create
a prototype subsystem board. The main objective was to give to a group of students
an early version of a blank PCB used on this project. With the aim to start the parallel
development and prototyping of other subsystems not covered in this work. In essence,
it was necessary to create a rapid prototyping platform that is compatible with the layout
and the connectors of the proposed CubeSat platform.
In this section, it is going to be described the design process of the prototype subsystem.
Starting by writing the requirements, followed by the block diagram, and ending with the
PCB design.
The main requirements of this subsystem are:
• The subsystem needed to adhere to the PCB layout and connectors of the CubeSat
platform. This would ensure that it could be seamlessly integrated into the overall
system.
• The subsystem had to be capable of communicating with other subsystems. Specif­
ically, it needed to be equipped with the ability to transmit and receive CAN mes­
sages, enabling it to participate in the networked communication within the CubeSat.
• The design had to incorporate a microcontroller. It was essential that the inputs and
outputs of this microcontroller were accessible, allowing for easy interfacing with
other components.
• The design had to provide sufficient space for the implementation of sensors. This
would allow for the collection of data from the CubeSat’s environment, which is a
crucial aspect of many CubeSat missions.
To meet these requirements, we proposed a solution that is very similar to the design of the
previously introduced COM subsystem, but without the inclusion of the radio transceiver.
Instead, our design incorporates a breadboard, providing a flexible platform for connecting
and soldering sensors. This design choice allows for the easy addition and modification of
sensors, making the subsystem highly adaptable to the specific needs of different Cube­
Sat missions.
Furthermore, we have chosen to use the same family of microcontrollers across the de­
sign. This decision adds a layer of reliability to the system, as using microcontrollers from
the same family ensures compatibility and reduces hardware conflicts. Additionally, it pro­
vides a better user experience for students involved in the project. By prototyping with
hardware that is going to be in the final system, students can gain practical experience
and a deeper understanding of the system’s operation. This approach not only enhances
the educational value of the project but also contributes to the overall robustness and
reliability of the final CubeSat system.
4.6.1
Prototype subsystem block diagram
The block diagram is shown in figure 4.15. The microcontroller is connected to the different
subsystems using the PC104 bus through the CAN bus. All the available inputs/outputs
of the ESP32S3 are available for the user, and allocated in the breadboard. One feature
of the ESP32 is that the General Purpose Input and Output (GPIO) can adopt different
functions. As an example, it can be configured a GPIO as an I2C interface in different
pins of the chip. This maximizes the flexibility of this subsystem.

4.7
On-Board Computer
The On­Board Computer (OBC) of this CubeSat educational platform is based on the
OBC of the DTUSAT­3 CubeSat which uses a male 40­pin connector, DF17(4.0)­40DP­
0.5V(57), in order to be connected to a backplane. To integrate this system into the ed­
ucational platform it is necessary to design a PCB that adapts the OBC of DTUSAT­3 to
the PC104 connector. Moreover, the master thesis developed by Blanca Alejo [26] was
focused on the design and the hardware manufacturing aspects, while the software im­
plementation of the hardware remains pending. To enable the detection of the various
peripherals, the installation of firmware and the Linux boot process will be necessary.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
37
==PAGE:51==
Figure 4.17: Manufactured PCB of the prototype subsystem
4.7.1
DTUSAT to PC104 adapter
In section 3.2 we discussed the different types of connectors in CubeSat. While DTUSAT­
3 is based on a 40­pin connector, this educational platform is based on a PC104 connector
in which only 52 pins are being used. This decision leads us to the necessity of creating
an adapter between the two types of physical interfaces.
Each subsystem has a header connector, DF17(4.0)­40DP­0.5V(57), and the carrier board
has a receptacle connector, DF17(4.0)­40DS­0.5V(57). The distribution of the signals
over the connector is described in figure 4.19. Each subsystem drives only 3.3V, and in
the case of the OBC, which is needed to supply 5 V to the OSD335x­SM SoC, there is
mounted a DC­DC Step up. It is intended to drive 3 UART connections, 2 I2C connections,
a CAN bus, a JTAG programming interface, and 5 signals for a shift register. In addition,
there is room for additional signal lines with 7 To­Be­Defined ports. The shift­register sig­
nals are used for multiplexing the signals of the different subsystems connected to the
carrier board. The main feature of this connector is the high amount of communication
interfaces and the low area it uses.
Figure 4.18: Left: Connector used at DTUSAT [40]. Right: A proposed connector for this
project
The two physical interfaces, from DTUSAT and the one proposed in this work, have some
similarities. Both of them use CAN and i2C buses, and 3V power pins. The GPIO pins
of the OBC are placed on the pins To­Be­Defined pins of the PC104 because there are
38
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:52==
not used by the OBC yet. It has been used solder­bridges for the different pins in order
to give the possibility to cut the lines if it is necessary. The interconnection of the different
signals of the DTUSAT­OBC and the PC104 is shown in figure 4.21.
Figure 4.19: DTUSAT connector pinout
Figure 4.20: Proposed PC104 pinout
Figure 4.21: Interconnection of DTUSAT and the proposed PC104 connector
4.7.2
Device Tree configuration
The OBC designed by Blanca Alejo [26] has some missing steps to have a fully integrated
system, especially on the Linux firmware. As discussed in section 3.5.1, it is necessary
to specify to the Kernel Linux the connected devices on the system, the layout of the
multiplexed pins, and some other essential functions. The main problem came when
connecting the CAN network to the OBC, CAN controller/transceiver was not configured
and it was not reachable by the Operating System. For the development of this master
thesis, it was necessary to configure the Kernel Linux in order to be able to use the CAN
transceiver. In this section, we are going to explain the necessary steps to configure
DTUSAT peripherals with the device tree on Linux.
The first step is to configure the EEPROM, described in section 3.5.1. The EEPROM
memory connected to the I2C0 bus need to have the information of the board ID so as
to select the correct overlays by the U­Boot. By default it is not possible to modify the
EEPROM memory because is write­protected, it is necessary to connect the pad labeled
as WP to GND, as shown in figure 4.22. After this temporal modification, we would be
able to modify the information of the EEPROM.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
39
==PAGE:53==
Figure 4.22: EEPROM write­protection pad connected to GND in DTUSAT OBC
The boot­up process of the BeagleBoard can be interrupted before the U­Boot startup
when is connected to UART. This UART interface is meant for debugging purposes and
lets us modify the content of the EEPROM. It is necessary to use an external UART to
USB connected to the UART0 bus of the board, placed on pins 2 and 4 of the 40­pin
connector of DTUSAT. We need to open a serial connection of 115200 baud rate, the
output is shown in figure 4.23. Pressing SPACE we would interrupt the autoboot.
Figure 4.23: Startup output of the OBC when connected through UART
From the output, we can notice some important things, first that we are having some errors
with some peripherals, and the reason is that they are not correctly configured on the
EEPROM. On this terminal interface, we can use the command i2c to fill the information
of some registers. We need to add the information shown in Appendix D. To verify that
the EEPROM is correctly configured, we can display the information of the registers of
the I2C0 bus, and the output should be like the figure 4.24.
After verifying that the EEPROM memory has the correct information, we can de­solder
40
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:54==
Figure 4.24: Content of the EEPROM memory of the OBC
the temporal jumper connected to the WP. The original design of the OBC is meant to
use the low power mode on the CAN transceiver, however, in this design, we are going
to use the high­speed mode because the power is not a limitation on this design. It is
necessary to remove the resister R13 and to connect Rs (pin 8) of the CAN transceiver
(U4) to GND, which could be the shield pin of the 40­pin connector. This way the OBC
of DTUSAT is running in high speed mode. Another optional modification is to power the
CAN transceiver through the 3.3V of the CPU, and not from the 40­pin connector, this is
especially useful for debugging purposes. For this purpose, we need to connect the Vcc
(pin 3) of the can transceiver (U4) to the +3.3V output of the OSD335x. The connections
are shown in figure 4.25.
Figure 4.25: Re­wiring of the CAN transceiver to work on high­speed mode and connected
to 3.3V
As described in section 3.5.1, where we described the Linux device tree, it is required to
create the device tree overlay with the information of our pins, and configure the multiplex
of them. The information is found on technical notes of the AM335x processor [29]. The
necessary information to create the device tree overlay has been collected in the table
4.3. The first column is the net name of the OBC DTUSAT schematic, please refer to it in
the appendix F. The second column is the physical pin name of the AM335X, where the
information is extracted from the datasheet of the processor on Table 4­2 [41]. The third
column is the multiplexing mode number extracted from the same table. The last column
is the register offset used to identify the pin, extracted from Table 9­10 of the technical
user manual of the AM335X [29].
The last step is to generate the overlay, compile it, and add it to the U­Boot process. The

4.7.3
On-Board computer software
When the OBC boots, it runs a service that configures the CAN controller to operate
with an external transceiver and then it runs the Python script listed on J. This piece of
software serves as a guide about how to receive the information from a sensor placed
on a different subsystem, and then send the information to the COM subsystem. In the
example proposed in this work, the OBC is receiving incoming CAN messages from the
Prototype Subsystem that is sending the information of a sample sensor (the temperature
sensor BME280). In this section, we will describe how it works.
The software of the OBC runs the library python­can, which connects to the CAN controller
with the driver SocketCAN. With this library, it is simple to receive and send messages
over the CAN network using Python. Another Python library used in this code is construct,
a tool for the construction and deconstruction of data structures in a declarative fashion.
It can create binary data structures similar to C but on Python, making it possible to build
and parse in an easy­to­understand way. It is used for encapsulating and parsing the data
structures of CFP and CCSDS.
The functionality of the software is shown in figure 4.26 with a block diagram. Firstly
it is read the incoming CAN messages from the Prototype Subsystem, reads the CAN
packets, and reassembly the CFP message containing the data of the temperature sensor.
Then is printed the data on the command line to check that the data is flowing correctly.
Finally, it generates a CCSDS packet with the received information of the sensor and it
is encapsulated in a CFP packet with the destination the COM SS. As we described in
2https://github.com/beagleboard/bb.org­overlays.git
42
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:56==
section 4.5.3, this information is going to be received by the COM subsystem and sent
via radio to the ground station.
Figure 4.26: Block diagram of the OBC software

4.8
Ground Segment
This section is intended to show the realized work regarding the Ground Segment. In
section 3.7 was defined the requirements for the Ground Segment. A complete TMTC was
defined in this work following the CCSDS standard and the XTCE standard. Moreover, a
integration of the Satellite Ground Software was created using Ball Cosmos.
4.8.1
Ground segment block diagram
The ground segment system of a typical space mission generally has an antenna, a radio
transceiver, and the ground control composed of a computer. This CubeSat simulation
has the same elements as a real mission but in a simpler manner. The different blocks
are described in figure 4.29.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
43
==PAGE:57==
Figure 4.28: Manufactured PCB of the On­Board Computer (OBC) subsystem
The ground segment is the same PCB as the COM subsystem but connected to a com­
puter. This is due to the fact that the transceiver is for LoRa communications, as in the
satellite. The microcontroller interface the RFM95 and the computer. The software run­
ning on the ESP32­S3 microcontroller is listed in appendix K. The C code makes the
microcontroller to act as a relay from the UART interface to the LoRa transceiver in both
directions. The received information in the computer (PC) needs to be piped from the
UART to the TCP/IP socket so that COSMOS ground segment software can receive it.
This is done by the block listed in yellow, UART2TCP.py, which is explained in the next
subsection.
Figure 4.29: Ground segment block diagram
4.8.2
UART to TCP/IP
The data is received by the ESP32­S3 of the Ground segment by a UART interface. The
Ball COSMOS software can use multiple communication interfaces, those are TCP/IP
server, TCP/IP client, UDP and Serial [33]. This ground station software is big and is
recommended to be installed with docker in a virtualized environment. It is developed
with a cloud mindset to be deployed using microservices, allowing it to be used on a
server with multiple remote clients. For this reason, the serial interface is not easy to
configure, because it is needed to pipe the serial ports to the virtual docker containers. In
the scenario we have, with a receiver transmitting the data over a serial link to the ground
segment computer, it makes sense to use the serial interface of Ball COSMOS, however,
it was not possible to implement it and there are more advantages by using a middleware
software with TCP/IP. The proposed architecture for this ground station segment is to use
Python middleware software that pipes the incoming UART information to TCP/IP socket
to the ground segment software. This way we can send efficiently the received data
44
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:58==
of the satellite to Ball COSMOS in a virtualized environment without the use of UART
communication.
Using a middleware software that pipes the UART data to TCP/IP gives new opportunities
that are worth mentioning. Firstly it adds a layer of modularity because the receiver part
can be detached from the ground segment software. This architecture makes it possible
to have a small receiver that sends the information to the cloud, stored in the cloud, and
then it can be reviewed by multiple users remotely.
4.8.3

As described in section 3.7.2, the ground station software will be based on Ball COS­
MOS software [33]. A CubeSat dashboard has been developed for this project in order
to decode incoming telemetry and also to send telecommands. This dashboard provides
different user interfaces such as telemetry graphing and a basic telemetry database. This
section presents to the reader the configuration of the software Ball COSMOS.
In order to connect to the target, the creation of an interface file is necessary. This doc­
ument defines the protocol used in the communication, i.e. TCP/IP, and some required
parameters, such as target IP or port. It is necessary to configure the interface to be able
to read CCSDS packets, as illustrated in the listing 4.1.

5
Results
The mechanical structure of the CubeSat was crafted using a 3D printer with PETG ma­
terial. This structure is held together using 24 M3 screws, 28 threaded inserts, and 4
threaded rods, each 90mm in length. To embed the threaded inserts into the 3D­printed
parts, a soldering iron was employed. For demonstration purposes, the prototype is en­
cased with six Plexiglas walls to shield the internal electronics, as shown in figure 5.1.
Excluding these Plexiglas walls, the structure weighs 89.54 grams. The combination of
threaded rods and the design of the subsystems lends structure robustness and solidity.
Given its strength, coupled with its rapid, cost­effective, and straightforward production
process, this design proves to be highly suitable for CubeSat applications.
Figure 5.1: Assembled structure printed with PETG filament
The electronics for this project were assembled at the DTUSAT facilities using hand sol­
dering techniques. The components were provided by DTUSAT laboratories and the com­
pany supporting this project, FaunaPhotonics. As depicted in figure 5.2, the various sub­
systems were assembled. From left to right, the prototype subsystem, the communica­
tions subsystem, and the On­Board computer subsystem. To further enhance accessibility
and transparency, each PCB has labeled a QR code of the source code repositories.
To facilitate testing, it became necessary to construct a rudimentary Electronic Power Sub­
system (EPS) capable of supplying power to the various subsystems. As the design of a
EPS is out of the scope of this project, a provisional solution was adopted. An empty pcb
of the prototype subsystem board was equipped with a battery and a voltage regulator.
This modified PCB effectively powers the different subsystems with 3.3 Volts, as depicted
in figure 5.3. With this configuration, the CubeSat can operate continuously for approx­
imately 3.5 hours, sufficient to demonstrate its core functionalities. The comprehensive
design and implementation of a dedicated EPS remain on the agenda for future work.
To demonstrate the design’s potential, a prototype subsystem was constructed, incorpo­
rating a basic temperature sensor, the BMP280. This setup aims to transmit real­time
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
47
==PAGE:61==
Figure 5.2: The different subsystems of the satellites soldered.
Figure 5.3: Temporary Electronic Power Subsystem for testing
sensor data, thereby validating the prototype’s operational efficacy. The sensor inter­
faces with the microcontroller via I2C and transmits its readings to the other subsystems
over the CAN network every five seconds. The software of this subsystem is listed in the
appendix L, written for the Arduino framework using the libraries created for this master
thesis. Figure 5.4 shows this subsystem with the sensor placed on the breadboard. The
seamless integration of the sensor highlights the design’s adaptability and its suitability
as an educational tool, facilitating easy extensions for diverse applications.
After assembling all the different subsystems we have a full demonstration of a CubeSat,
shown in figure 5.5. Each subsystem is securely anchored to the threaded rods, which
are integrated into the structure, ensuring overall stability and rigidity. The efficient in­
tegration of the different subsystems underscores the professionalism of the standards
followed during the design process. With a total mass of 475.67 grams, it exceeds all
initial expectations.
The software of each subsystem was described in detail in the past sections, however, it is
going to be illustrated as a high­level overview of the software design. Figure 5.6 illustrates
the interaction between different subsystems, particularly when the prototype subsystem
48
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:62==
Figure 5.4: Prototype subsystem populated with a BMP280 temperature sensor
with the temperature sensor transmits its data. The prototype subsystem with the BMP
is configured to send the telemetry over CAN every 5 seconds. The OBC receives the
information and creates a CCSDS packet with the data. The CCSDS packet is sent to the
COM subsystem that relays the data over Lora. The ground segment received the data,
and forwards it to the TCP/IP socket which is connected to the COSMOS software. The
software BALL COSMOS receives the data and plots it. Upon initialization, the satellite
starts the telemetry transmission over LoRa. Concurrently, once operational, the ground
station begins to receive and plot the incoming telemetry.
The ground station software, BALL COSMOS, is capable to receive, store, and plotting
the telemetry among other functionalities. Figure 5.7 shows a capture of some telemetry
received in the ground station. It shows the temperature reading of the sensor BME280.
This graph, populated with telemetry data, confirms that all components of the system are
functioning cohesively, ensuring a distributed system that reliably transmits telemetry.

Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:66==
6
Conclusions
The objective of this Master Thesis was to design and implement a 1U CubeSat Simulation
platform following the industry standards on CubeSat development. From an educational
point of view, this work should be a realistic approximation of a CubeSat with its main
functions and subsystems.
First of all, the On­Board Computer (OBC) of DTUSAT 3 was implemented into the project,
which required the design of a Printed Circuit Board (PCB) that would adapt the connector
to the PC104 CubeSat standard. The integration of the OBC’s peripherals with the Linux
Kernel was a critical step, necessitating the creation of a software implementation for the
OBC. This accomplishment is remarkable for this project, and for the development of the
DTUSAT because it validates the OBC with different subsystems, by the integration of the
software in a real scenario.
The second significant accomplishment was the development of the Communication (COM)
subsystem, which is based on LoRa technology and the ESP32S3 microcontroller. This
subsystem counts with a CAN transceiver and, is capable to send telemetry over LoRa,
which can be controlled using the CAN protocol. In parallel to this development, to fa­
cilitate the efficient communication between different subsystems over a CAN network, a
library was implemented for the Fragmentation and reassembly of the CAN messages.
This resulted in a communication subsystem capable of sending telemetry and telecom­
mands over radio and exchange data with other subsystems via the CAN network.
The third phase of the project involved the development of a prototype subsystem, which
served as a playground for implementing various sensors in an easy manner. This subsys­
tem is composed by a ESP32S3 microcontroller, a CAN transceiver, and a breadboard.
As an example, the temperature sensor BMP280 was implemented, and a software was
created with the intention to send the data of the sensors to the other subsystems.
The fourth phase of the project is the implementation of the ground station based on
the open­source software COSMOS. The ground segment was designed to receive and
transmit information to the ground software, enabling it to plot different sensor data and
send telecommands to the satellite.
The fifth and final phase involved the development of a mechanical structure that adheres
to the CubeSat standard. All the developed subsystems were implemented into this struc­
ture, demonstrating the successful cooperation of all the different subsystems. This work
could serve as the foundation for future CubeSat projects.
In conclusion, this project successfully met the initial requirements and objectives set out
at the beginning. The resulting CubeSat Simulation platform is not only a realistic approxi­
mation of a CubeSat, but it also provides a valuable educational tool for understanding the
main functions and subsystems of a CubeSat. Furthermore, it lays a solid foundation for
future CubeSat developments, demonstrating the feasibility and efficiency of the design
and implementation processes used in this project.
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
53
==PAGE:67==
54
Design and Implementation of a 1U CubeSat Simulation Platform
==PAGE:68==
7
Future work
There are some parts of this master thesis project that could be improved in the future,
and other parts that are missing. This chapter discusses these issues and opportunities
and summarizes tasks for future work.
7.0.1
Electric Power Subsystem design
For this Master Thesis it was used a temporary Electric Power Subsystem based on a
battery and a buck converter. This solution helped us to test the CubeSat without being
connected to the computer, however, is far from a proper Electric Power Subsystem. It is
vital to design a complete system that can be used on the next revisions of this platform.
The Electric Power Subsystem (EPS) is a critical component of any CubeSat, responsi­
ble for power generation, storage, and distribution. For instance, the integration of more
efficient solar cells, advanced battery technologies, and smarter power management al­
gorithms could significantly improve the CubeSat’s power capabilities. Additionally, the
implementation of fault detection, isolation, and recovery (FDIR) strategies could increase
the robustness and reliability of the EPS.
7.0.2
Develop a new On-Board computer
The On­Board Computer (OBC) of the CubeSat, based on the OBC of DTUSAT­3, has
served its purpose well in this project. However, there are known issues and limitations
with the current design that could be addressed in a future version. A new OBC could be
developed with a more powerful processor, increased memory capacity, and enhanced
communication capabilities. Additionally, the software could be optimized for better per­
formance and reliability. The development of a new OBC would not only improve the
functionality of the CubeSat but also provide an opportunity to incorporate the latest ad­
vancements in microprocessor technology.
7.0.3
Validate the functionality of the design
While the CubeSat Simulation platform has been tested in a laboratory setting, a more
realistic validation of its functionality could be achieved through a high­altitude balloon
flight. This would expose the CubeSat to conditions more similar to those it would en­
counter in space, such as low pressure and temperature extremes. Data collected during
the balloon flight could be used to verify the performance of the CubeSat’s subsystems
and to identify any issues that need to be addressed before a potential space deployment.
7.0.4
Create a science case for students
The CubeSat Simulation platform has significant potential as an educational tool. A future
task could be the development of an educational workshop or curriculum based around
the CubeSat. This could involve students in the assembly and testing of the CubeSat, as
well as in the analysis of data collected during laboratory tests or balloon flights. Such a
hands­on learning experience would not only enhance students’ understanding of space
technology but also provide valuable feedback for the further development of the CubeSat.
It would also serve as a validation of the educational potential of the CubeSat Simulation
platform.

need to close the loop with the 
solderbridge
RS: Mode select pin: strong pulldown to GND = high speed 
mode, strong pullup to VCC = low power mode, 10-kΩ to 100-kΩ 
pulldown to GND = slope control mode

//

// Created by Fran Acién 2023-05-22

#include "CFP.h"
5
6
7
void parseCFPHeader(uint32_t id, CAN_CFP_HEADER* packet){
8
packet->source = ( id >> CFP_SOURCE_OFFSET) & CFP_SOURCE_MASK;
9
packet->destination = ( id >> CFP_DESTINATION_OFFSET) & CFP_DESTINATION_MASK
;
10
packet->type = (id >> CFP_TYPE_OFFSET) & CFP_TYPE_MASK;
11
packet->remain = (id >> CFP_REMAIN_OFFSET) & CFP_REMAIN_MASK;
12
packet->id = id & CFP_ID_MASK;
13
14
printCFPHeader(packet);
15
}
16
17
void printCFPHeader(CAN_CFP_HEADER* packet){
18
Serial.printf("SOURCE: %d\n", packet ->source);
19
Serial.printf("DESTINATION: %d\n", packet->destination);
20
Serial.printf("TYPE: %d\n", packet->type);
21
Serial.printf("REMAIN: %d\n", packet ->remain);
22
Serial.printf("ID: %d\n", packet ->id);
23
}
24
25
CAN_CFP_HEADER createCFPHeader(uint8_t source , uint8_t destination , uint8_t
type, uint8_t remain,
26
uint16_t id){
27
CAN_CFP_HEADER cfp_id = {source, destination , type, remain , id};
28
29
return cfp_id;
30
}
31
32

void sendCFP(CAN_CFP_DATA frame_data , uint8_t source , uint8_t destination ,
